[1] The heart is tied to Palma
[2] 1
[3] In the morning, Mr. Parker Paine took the steamship departing from Barcelona to Majorca Island [1]. As soon as he arrived on the island, Mr. Parker Paine was given a taste of what was to come - all the hotels were fully booked! The best accommodation he could find was a stuffy little room above a hotel courtyard in the city center. Mr. Parker Paine was clearly not impressed, but the hotel owner did not seem to care.
[4] "What else do you want?" the hotel owner shrugged.
[5] This was peak season in Palma! The island was bustling with business, just like the British and Americans who chose to visit Majorca in the winter! The place was packed, and it was uncertain whether Mr. Parker Paine, the British gentleman, could find a place to stay - unless he was willing to consider the exorbitant prices of the Boutique Hotel of Bunyanyola [3].
[6] Mr. Parker Paine had some coffee and bread in the temporary hotel he was staying in and then went out. He had intended to admire the magnificent architecture of the cathedral, but he found himself without the mood.
[7] Later, he met a Spanish taxi driver who was not only friendly but could also speak a bit of French. They talked about the surrounding areas of Majorca Island, including Bunyanyola, Soller, Alcudia, and Palma - all of which had great hotels but high prices.
[8] Mr. Parker Paine couldn't help but show interest in the hotel prices.
[9] From the driver's mouth, he learned that the hotels were all overpriced - did nobody know that the British came to this place because of the affordable and fair prices?
[10] Mr. Parker Paine did not deny it and continued to inquire about the hotel prices in Bunyanyola.
[11] "Exorbitant!"
[12] "Excellent - but how much?"
[13] The driver finally gave in to Parker Pane's insistence and told him the exact amount.
[14] However, for Parker Pane, who had recently stayed in the luxury hotels of Jerusalem and Egypt, this price was not much.
[15] Thus, Parker Pane decided to look for accommodation himself. After loading all his luggage onto the car, they set off. They initially circled the island in search of cheaper small inns, but eventually settled on Palma.
[16] However, they did not reach these mansions, as they followed the narrow streets and winding coastline of Bolinas, arriving at the Pino d'Oro Hotel - a small seaside inn. In the morning mist, the scenery around the hotel resembled a meticulously crafted Japanese woodblock print. Mr. Parker Pyne almost immediately took a liking to the inn and signaled the driver to stop. He approached the painted gate with a sense of hope.
[17] The innkeepers were an elderly couple who did not speak English or French, but this did not hinder their communication. Soon, Mr. Parker Pyne was handed the key to a seaside room. After helping Mr. Parker Pyne unload his luggage, the relieved taxi driver wished his client well, took his fare, and enthusiastically greeted him in Spanish before driving off.
[18] Mr. Parker Pyne glanced at his watch and saw that it was still early - only 10:15 - so he walked directly to the terrace, where he ordered another coffee and bread for breakfast.
[19] The terrace had four tables, one for clearing dishes and one that Mr. Parker Pyne was using. The other two tables were occupied. The table closest to him was a family of four Germans, consisting of a mother and father and their two daughters. Further away, in the corner of the terrace, a British mother and son were clearly visible.
[20] The British mother was probably around fifty-five years old, with hair just the right amount of silver - even the unfashionable tweed jacket and skirt could not hide her sharp wit - a calm demeanor that immediately revealed her as a British woman who had traveled extensively and seen the world.
[21] The young man sitting across from her was around twenty-five years old, neither tall nor short, and not particularly handsome but not ordinary either, with a typical appearance for his class and age. It was clear that their mother-son relationship was excellent - the young man not only joked with his mother but also helped her with various tasks.
[22] While speaking to his son, the British woman's gaze met Mr. Parker Pane's, and although good manners allowed her to quickly avert her gaze, Mr. Parker Pane knew that he had been noticed. He knew that once he was recognized as a British person, there would be some superficial small talk that would follow.
[23] Mr. Parker Pane was not particularly opposed to this. Although his British compatriots, both men and women, would make him feel a bit bored, he still hoped to treat others as kindly as possible - especially in a small inn where they would inevitably cross paths.
[24] He was certain that the British woman in front of him was an expert in hotel etiquette.
[25] The boy stood up, joked around for a bit, and then went back into the room. After his son left, the mother picked up her handbag and a few letters, found a chair facing the sea, and settled down to read her copy of The Daily Mail.
[26] Mr. Parker Pyn sat behind her, and when he drained the last drop of coffee in his cup, his gaze inadvertently swept over her back. To his surprise, this casual glance made him feel as if his blood suddenly froze. He had a premonition – a premonition that his peaceful and leisurely vacation might end soon! Years of rich experience told him what this stiff and tense back meant – without looking at the face, he could tell that the owner of this back was making great efforts to hold back tears.
[27] Like a cautious animal that needs to be alert to predators, Mr. Parker Pyn carefully returned to his room. Half an hour later, he received a notice from the front desk to sign.
[28] He signed his name carefully in the register – C. Parker Pyn, London.
[29] He then glanced up a few lines and saw a few names: Mrs. R. Chester, Mr. Basil Chester – Holm Park, Devonshire.
[30] Mr. Parker Pyn then took out a pen and quickly added a few strokes to his name, making it difficult to recognize – Christopher Pyn.
[31] This way, even if Mrs. R. Chester had any unpleasant matters in Bolonia, she would not easily find Mr. Parker Pyne.
[32] Mr. Parker Pyne had often wondered why many people he met abroad knew his name and had seen his advertisements, while in Britain, where thousands of people read The Times every day, he was still told by some people that they had never heard of Mr. Parker Pyne. He then realized that when people are abroad, they read newspapers more carefully, not missing any news, even advertisements.
[33] Several times before, Mr. Parker Pyne had to interrupt his holiday to deal with various matters, ranging from murder cases to planned kidnappings. He decided to enjoy his trip to Mallorca this time. However, his intuition told him that his peaceful holiday would be broken by the melancholy mother.
[34] Mr. Parker Pyne was comfortable staying at the Pinocchio Hotel. There was a larger hotel nearby called Mariposa, where many British people lived, and there was also an artists' gathering place. Along the coast, you could walk into a fishing village with a few small shops and a popular cocktail bar. Girls in colorful scarves walked around in loose pants, and young men with long hair and berets gathered together to chat about art. Everything exuded tranquility and joy.
[35] The next day, Mrs. Chester approached Mr. Parker Pyne several times, and their conversations were either about the scenery or the weather. Afterward, she chatted with a German lady about knitting, and then went to discuss politics with two Danish gentlemen who had already walked for ten hours after waking up early.
[36] Meanwhile, Mr. Parker Pyne developed a great liking for Basil Chester during this time. Basil called him "sir" and always behaved modestly in front of him. Sometimes after dinner, the three British people would gather together to drink coffee.
[37] On the fourth day, when Basil left early, Mr. Parker Pyne had the opportunity to talk to his mother alone. They talked about flowers and gardening, lamented the plight of the pound, and marveled at how much the franc had risen. They also complained about not being able to find good afternoon tea.
[38] From that day on, Mr. Parker Pyne noticed that Mrs. Chesterfield always tried to hide her unease at first, but soon she would happily return to the previous topic.
[39] Gradually, Mrs. Chesterfield began to tell Mr. Parker Pyne about Basil - how outstanding he was at school - "he was ranked sixth"; how popular he was; how proud his father would have been if he were still alive; and how grateful she was that Basil had never "gone wild".
[40] "I always advise him to spend more time with young people, but he seems to really enjoy spending time with me," Mrs. Chesterfield said, a little embarrassed but also happy.
[41] However, Mr. Parker Pyne did not offer his usual quick-witted response this time.
[42] "Oh! There are many young people here - not in the hotel, but around the area," Mr. Parker Pyne said.
[43] But upon saying this, he noticed Mrs. Chester's unusual reaction.
[44] "There are indeed many artists in the area," she said coldly.
[45] This might be due to her conservatism - true art is another matter, but it certainly does not equate to the excuses young people often fabricate for their idleness - and some girls do indulge in excessive drinking.
[46] "Mr. Parker Pyncheon, I am very glad to meet you here – especially from my mother's point of view. She enjoys our evening conversations with you very much," Basil said to Mr. Parker Pyncheon the next day.
[47] "What did you do when you first arrived here?"
[48] "We often played Piquet [4] then."
[49] "Understood."
[50] "Of course, people get tired of playing that eventually. I have made some friends here – a very active group. But my mother doesn't approve of them –" Basil chuckled, thinking he had made a joke. "My mother is very conservative – she can't even accept girls wearing pants!"
[51] "Indeed," Mr. Pike said in agreement.
[52] "I told her – one must keep up with the times... our girls at home are too dull..."
[53] "I understand," Mr. Pike thought to himself, feeling intrigued.
[54] Mr. Pike's worst fears were realized as he and Mrs. Chestefield met his acquaintance – a flamboyant woman who lived at the nearby Mariposa Hotel.
[55] The woman spotted Mr. Pike and exclaimed:
[56] "Well, if it isn't Parker Pynne! And Adela Chester! You know each other? Oh, know each other? You stay at the same hotel? Adela, he's a one-of-a-kind, genuine marvel of the century—he can fix all your problems! Don't you know? Have you heard of him? Have you seen his ads? 'You have a problem? Call Parker Pynne.' He's a man of all trades. He can make bickering couples reconcile—he can arrange an unforgettable adventure that will reignite your passion for life. As I said, this man is a marvel!"
[57] Parker Pynne's admirer went on and on, leaving him no choice but to interject. He didn't like the way Mrs. Chester looked at him, and he couldn't bear watching her walk back to the beach and continue chatting with the chatty admirer.
[58] Things developed much faster than Parker Pynne had anticipated. Later that evening, after coffee, Mrs. Chester began to inquire.
[59] "Mr. Pynne, could you join me in the little salon? I have something I'd like to discuss with you."
[60] Parker Pynne complied, deferentially.
[61] Just as Mrs. Chesters was about to lose control of her emotions, they entered the salon. As soon as the door closed behind them, tears welled up in Mrs. Chesters' eyes.
[62] "Mr. Parker Pynne, it's my son. You have to save him. We have to save him. My heart is breaking!"
[63] "Dear madam, I'm just an outsider -"
[64] "Nina Whitchell said you're all-powerful. She said I should have faith in you. She suggested I tell you everything - then you'll know what to do."
[65] Despite his constant cursing of Nina Whitchell's meddling, Mr. Parker Pynne managed to keep his emotions in check.
[66] "So, let's see. I assume it's about a girl?"
[67] "Has he mentioned her?"
[68] "Only indirectly."
[69] "That girl is terrible, drinking and cursing—never wearing proper clothes," Mrs. Chester's grievances poured out like a broken dam, "She has an older sister who lives here—married to a Dutch artist. These people are beyond help; half of them are living in sin. Basil is not the same anymore; he used to be so quiet, only serious topics interested him, and he even wanted to become an archaeologist—"
[70] "That's fine," Mr. Parker Pynchon agreed. "Let it be."
[71] "What do you mean?"
[72] "For young men, being only interested in serious topics is not good for their health. He should pursue a girl like an idiot."
[73] "Mr. Pike Paine, please be serious."
[74] "I am serious. Is the girl you're talking about the one you had tea with yesterday?"
[75] Mr. Pike Paine did remember the girl with the red lips – she wore a pair of gray flannel pants and a loose red scarf around her neck – but what was even more memorable was that she was drinking cocktails instead of tea.
[76] "Did you see her? Horrible! She's not the kind of girl Basil wants."
[77] "But you haven't given him any chance to like a girl."
[78] "Me?"
[79] "Basil has grown accustomed to having you around! That's not good! But I dare say he'll overcome it - as long as you don't interfere."
[80] "You haven't realized yet. He's going to marry a girl named Betty Gregg - they're engaged."
[81] "Has it come to that?"
[82] "Yes, Mr. Pike, you must do something. You can't let my son get into such a disastrous marriage! Otherwise, his whole life will be ruined."
[83] "No one's life can be ruined by someone else, unless they allow it themselves."
[84] "But Basil is different." Mrs. Chester insisted.
[85] "I'm not worried about Basil."
[86] "But aren't you worried about the girl?"
[87] "No, I'm worried about you. You're abusing your parental authority."
[88] Mrs. Chesterton was taken aback by Mr. Parker Pyne's words for the first time.
[89] "What does a person's twenties to forties mean? It's a time when they need to be bound by human and emotional relationships. Life is just like that. But then they enter a new phase, when people start to think and observe life, discovering that others are also seeking their true selves, and life begins to truly have weight. Life is a process, not just a scene with you in it. Men and women are the same; no one can become a true self before the age of forty-five, so everyone still has a chance before then."
[90] "I've put so much into Basil. He's everything to me." Mrs. Chesterton said.
[91] "No, you shouldn't see him that way. And you have already tasted the bitterness of doing so. You can give him as much love as possible – but remember, you are Adela Chester, an independent person – not just Basil's mother."
[92] "If Basil's life is ruined, so is mine."
[93] Mr. Pike's words made him re-examine the woman in front of him – despite the fine lines on her face and the somber expression, there was still a charm to her that was evident. Mr. Pike couldn't help but feel a pang of sympathy.
[94] "I will find a way."
[95] When Mr. Pike found Basil, he realized that the other man was even more eager to express his point of view.
[96] "This whole thing is terrible. My mother is just...unredeemable - prejudiced, narrow-minded. She won't even try to understand how good Betty is."
[97] "How is Betty now?"
[98] Basil sighed.
[99] "Betty is very hard to convince! She would be so much easier if she could just be a little more laid back - like not wearing lipstick every day - and things would be easier. But the fact is, she always seems to want to appear very modern when my mother is around."
[100] Parker Pyncheon smiled slightly.
[101] "Betty and your mother are two of the people you love most in the world. I thought they would appreciate and love each other. You have a lot to learn, young man."
[102] "I hope you can come with me to see Betty and talk to her properly."
[103] Mr. Parker Pynne was waiting for this statement.
[104] Betty and her sister, sister-in-law lived in a dilapidated villa near the beach. Their simple life gave a refreshing feeling. All the furniture added up to only three chairs, a table, and a bed. There was a cupboard nailed to the wall, with cups and plates displayed openly. Hans was a young man with a golden buzz cut, who spoke fast and twisted English as he walked around the room. Compared to Betty Gregg, who had red hair, freckles, and a sharp gaze, her sister Stella seemed petite and fair-skinned. Mr. Parker Pynne noticed that she had almost no makeup, just like the day before at the Pinodoc Hotel.
[105] Betty served Mr. Parker Pynne a cocktail.
[106] "You got caught up in it?" She winked cunningly.
[107] Mr. Parker Pyn nodded.
[108] "So, which side are you on, old boy? The young lovers or the elderly opponents?"
[109] "Can I ask you a question first?"
[110] "Of course."
[111] "Didn't you go a bit overboard?"
[112] "Absolutely not," said Miss Gregg firmly, "but that old man really got under my skin. I was about to go mad. Basil has been pampered by her all these years – it's made him dull. He's not stupid, mind you, and that old woman always puts on airs and graces."
[113] "Well, that's not necessarily a bad thing. It's just not very 'in vogue' right now."
[114] "You mean like taking a Queen Anne chair from an antique shop and putting it in a Victorian room, then later saying, 'Isn't it amazing?'"
[115] "Something like that."
[116] "Maybe you're right. I should be honest. What really got under my skin was Basil – he was so worried about what impression he'd make on his mother. I've had enough. I feel like he'll give up on me – if his mother uses her influence on him."
[117] "Possibly," said Mr. Pike Paine, "if her tactics are effective."
[118] "Will you tell her what to do? She wouldn't think of it herself. She'd just oppose it, to no avail. But if you gave her a hint –" Betty Gregg bit her lip, looking at Mr. Pike Paine with her blue eyes. "Mr. Pike Paine, I've heard of you. You should have a good understanding of human nature. Do you think Basil and I should give it a try?"
[119] "You'll have to answer three questions first."
[120] "A compatibility test? No problem, go ahead."

[126] "Honestly, I prefer to go to bed early. By ten thirty at night, I start yawning—and I feel refreshed in the morning—but of course, I wouldn't admit that."
[127] "You two seem well-suited," Mr. Parker Pynne concluded.
[128] "What a shallow test," Betty said.
[129] "Not at all. I know of at least seven completely broken marriages where the husband or wife would stay up late into the night, while the other would fall asleep by nine thirty."

[133] Mr. Parker Pynne remained composed and did not respond to this.
[134] On Mrs. Chester's side, Mr. Parker Pynne always tried to reassure her as much as possible, but also often used vague phrases such as "engagement does not equal marriage." During this time, he spent a week in Soleil. He also advised Mrs. Chester to act more reluctantly and to appear to agree with difficulty.
[135] Mr. Parker Pynne enjoyed a week of vacation in Soleil, but upon his return, he found an unexpected development in the situation.
[136] As he entered the Pinewood Hotel, he saw Mrs. Chester and Miss Betsey drinking tea together. Miss Betsey's face, which was almost unmade up, looked pale in the face of the somewhat aggressive Mrs. Chester. If you looked closely, you could tell that she had been crying.
[137] When Mr. Parker Pyne walked in, both ladies greeted him politely, but none of them mentioned Basil.
[138] Suddenly, Miss Betsey seemed to be in pain, drawing in a sharp breath, causing Mr. Parker Pyne to turn his head in the direction of her gaze.
[139] They saw Basil approaching from the beach, but with a girl who was so beautiful that it took your breath away. Her slim figure was even more alluring against her dark skin. She was almost naked, wearing only a light blue chiffon, and her face was painted with orange-red lipstick and ochre foundation - although it looked heavy, it made her look more glamorous and modern. As for young Basil, his eyes barely looked anywhere else.
[140] "Basil, you're late," scolded Mrs. Chester. "Weren't you supposed to take Betsey to the McBar?"
[141] "It's all my fault," the girl said before introducing herself, "we were just taking a stroll." She then turned to Basil and said, "Darling—let's have some fun!"
[142] She kicked off her shoes, revealing her toes, which were painted the same shade of emerald green as her fingernails and trimmed neatly.
[143] "This island is so dreadful," she paid no attention to the other two women present and leaned slightly towards Mr. Parker Pyn, "I was about to die of boredom before I met Basil. He's such a charming fellow!"
[144] "Miss Ramona—this is Mr. Parker Pyn," Chester introduced.

[229] The steamship slowly pulled away from Palma. [285] Litcham Manor is one of the most famous ancient mansions in Britain. Its owner, Hubert Litcham Rock, is the last head of the family. Relatives tend to describe him as "Hubert the old man, you know, really should have been given a certificate. Poor old chap, a real nutcase."
[286] There is some truth to the exaggerated descriptions of Hubert Lichfield Rock. He is indeed an eccentric man. Although he is a talented musician, he has a volatile temper and is obsessed with his own reputation. Guests visiting the manor must respect his many prejudices, or they will never receive a second invitation.
[287] One of his prejudices is his music. If he plays for guests – which he often does in the evenings – the audience must remain absolutely silent. Whispering, rustling clothes, or even a movement might make him fly into a rage and leave, leaving the unfortunate guests without another invitation to the manor.
[288] His other rule is that the most important meal of the day must be served exactly on time. Breakfast doesn't matter, and you can come at midday if you like. Lunch is the same, just cold meat with slow-cooked fruit. Dinner, however, is a ceremony, a festival, with a Michelin-starred chef from a top hotel.
[289] At 8:05, the first bell rings. At 8:15, the second bell sounds, and the door is thrown open with a bang. The announcement of the start of dinner is made to the assembled guests, who file solemnly into the dining room in a long line. Anyone who dares to arrive late after the second bell will be thrown out of the manor and forever barred from returning.
[290] It's no wonder Joan Ashby is so anxious, and no wonder Harry Dallahouse is shocked to hear that the sacred dining ceremony tonight has been delayed by ten minutes. Although his relationship with his uncle is not too close, he still frequents Lichfield Manor, so he knows how unusual this is.

[930] Mr. Satterthwaite had made a "clucking" sound of annoyance twice. Regardless of whether his theory was true or not, he was increasingly convinced that cars nowadays were much more prone to breaking down than before. He only trusted the cars that had stood the test of time and could still function properly. They all had their quirks, but you knew them well and could provide them with the necessary maintenance and repairs. However, new cars were different! With all the new parts, different types of windows, and the new, beautiful wooden dashboard – but completely unfamiliar, your hands fumbling through the fog lights, windshield wipers, and the throttle, all the parts installed in places you were not used to. When your newly bought shiny car broke down, the words of the local mechanic made you even more annoyed: "The teething difficulties of the new car, just like a baby teething. The car is great, sir, these convertibles are great. All the latest parts, you know, there's always some trouble when a baby teeth, ha-ha! "As if the car was a baby with teeth coming in.
[931] However, Mr. Satterthwaite was already an elderly man at the time, and he very much hoped that a new car should have good performance. Through various tests and inspections, all the issues during the break-in period were taken care of before it reached the hands of the buyer.
[932] Mr. Satterthwaite drove to the countryside to visit friends on this weekend. Not long after leaving London, his new car started to show some issues, and it was now parked at a garage waiting for repairs. He had no idea how long it would take before he could hit the road again. His driver was negotiating with a mechanic. Mr. Satterthwaite sat there, trying to remain patient. He had confirmed with the host last night that he would arrive promptly for tea. He promised to arrive at Doverton Kingsburn Manor by four o'clock.
[933] He grumbled again with "clucking" sounds, trying to think of something pleasant. In this agitated state, constantly checking his watch and clucking repeatedly, he couldn't help but realize that the sound he made was very much like that of a happy hen calling out for laying eggs.
[934] Yes, think of something pleasant. Well, hadn't he noticed something while driving here? Not long ago, as he peered out the window, he saw something that filled him with joy and excitement. But he didn't have time to ponder it then, as the car's issues became more apparent, and they had to pull over at the nearest service station.
[935] What did he see then? On the left - no, on the right. Yes, on the left as they slowly drove through the countryside streets, he saw it, next to a post office. Yes, he was certain. Because seeing the post office's sign made him think of calling the Edison family to let them know he might be late. The post office, a rural post office. Next to it - yes, he was certain. If not, then it was just beyond the next house number. Something stirred memories from the past, and he wanted - what, exactly? Oh, heavens! He was about to remember. There was a mix of colors, several colors. Yes, one or several colors. Or a specific word. A precise word that stirred his memories, thoughts, joy, and excitement, recalling something vivid and real. He had not only seen and observed it with his heart. No, he had done more. He had participated. Participated in what? Why? And where? Everywhere. In his final thoughts, he quickly found the answer. Everywhere.
[936] Was it on an island? In Corsica? Watching the roulette wheel at Monte Carlo? In a country house? Everywhere. He had been to all these places, and with another person. Yes, with another person. Everything was connected to this person. He was about to remember, just as his thoughts were interrupted by the driver. He came to the window, and the mechanic pulled the tow rope behind him.
[937] "It won't be long, sir," the driver assured Mr. Sutcliffe in a light-hearted tone. "Ten minutes at most, and it won't be more."
[938] "It's not a big problem," the mechanic said in a low, gravelly country accent. "It's just the difficulties of the break-in period, like teething for an infant. You probably know."
[939] Mr. Sutcliffe didn't make a "cluck" sound this time. He gritted his teeth. This phrase he had often read in books, and now that he was older, he seemed to have gotten used to spitting it out from his slightly loose upper jaw. Yes, the discomfort of teething! Toothache. Biting. Bad teeth. He thought, a person's life is centered around teeth.
[940] "We're only a few miles from Doverton Kingsburn," the driver said. "They have a taxi. You can take a taxi, sir. I'll follow as soon as the car is repaired."
[941] "No!" Mr. Satterthwaite said.
[942] The words burst from his mouth, and both the driver and the mechanic were startled. Mr. Satterthwaite's eyes lit up, and his voice was clear and firm, and he finally remembered.
[943] "I intend," he said, "to walk along the path we came. When the car is fixed, you can come and pick me up there, at the Clown's Cafe, I think that's the name."
[944] "Sir, it's not a very nice place," the mechanic reminded him.
[945] "That's exactly why I want to go there," Mr. Satterthwaite said in an authoritative tone.
[946] He strode off briskly, leaving the two men staring after him.
[947] "I wonder what's got into him," said the driver. "I've never seen him like this."
[948] The village of Kingsburn Darcy did not live up to its name, which suggested an ancient and dignified character. It was a small village with just one street and a few houses. There were a few shops scattered about, some of which seemed to have been converted from residences or vice versa, lacking any semblance of an industrial society.
[949] The village was not old and did not boast much beauty, being rather plain and unremarkable. Mr. Satterthwaite wondered if it was precisely this simplicity that had caught his attention. Ah, he had reached the post office. It was a simple building housing newspapers and postcards, with a mailbox outside. Next to it, yes, there was a sign. The Clown's Cafe. Mr. Satterthwaite felt a sense of unease. After all, he was getting on in years. He couldn't help wondering why the name had unsettled him so. The Rainbow Cafe.
[950] The mechanic at the service station was right; this was not a place that would attract people to sit down and have a proper meal. Perhaps a quick bite or a cup of morning coffee. But why had he come here? He suddenly realized the reason. The cafe, or rather the establishment that sold coffee, was divided into two parts. One side had a few small tables and chairs for regular customers to dine, while the other side was a shop selling ceramics. It was not an antique shop, but rather a shop selling modern items, with a display of glass vases and mugs in the window. The shop featured a variety of tea sets, each with a different color: blue, red, yellow, green, pink, purple. It was a delightful display of colors, thought Mr. Satterthwaite. As the car slowly made its way down the sidewalk, searching for any signs of a repair shop or service station, it was no wonder that the shop's window display had caught his attention. The window featured a large card reading "Rainbow Tea Set."
[951] Of course, the word "Rainbow" had been deeply imprinted in Mr. Sattthwaite's mind, despite the distant memory and difficulty in recalling it. Bright colors. Colorful colors. He pondered, contemplated, and came up with a ridiculous yet thrilling idea: in some ways, it was calling him. This place was prepared especially for him. Perhaps his old friend, Harry Quinn, would come here for a meal or to buy a tea set. How long had it been since he had seen Mr. Quinn? Many years. It was on that day, he saw Mr. Quinn departing along a country lane called Lovers Lane [2]. He had always wanted to see Mr. Quinn again, at least once a year, possibly twice. But they hadn't met again.

This feeling came from the words Mr. Quinn would say. He might show him something, and Mr. Sattthwaite would have further thoughts about it. He would observe, imagine, and discover the truth. He also had a fondness for young people who were not yet established in the social world, those who had difficulties, were in love, unhappy, or needed help.

[1134] Now he felt there was something going on, some crisis, something that could affect everyone or some people. Berill Gillett, Mrs. Gillett, was tense, fidgeting. Tom? Tom was fine; he wouldn't be affected. He was lucky to have this beautiful woman, to have Duffton, to have a grandson, and everything would be Roland's when he died. It would all be Roland's. Was Tom hoping that Roland would marry Inez? Or was he worried about the close marriage between first cousins?
[1135] Mr. Satterthwaite pondered, even though historically there had been no ill effects from cousin marriages. "Let nothing happen," Mr. Satterthwaite said. "Let nothing happen. If something happens, I must stop it."
[1136] Really, his mind was full of crazy ideas. A peaceful scene. A tea set. Colorful teacups of various colors. He looked at the white clay pipe lying beside the red teacup. Beril Guillette said something to Timothy, who nodded and got up to walk towards the house. [1139] A table was filled with colorful teacups, and, oh, Timothy, his red hair shone in the sunlight. His red hair, as lustrous as Simon Guillette's. [1206] He looked surprised as he saw a tall, slender woman with fair complexion approaching him. She was wearing a light pearl-colored dress and was walking towards Mr. Satterthwaite. He stood rigidly, looking at her.
[1207] "Lily," he called, "Lily."
[1208] Now he could see it clearly. It was Lily who was approaching him. Although he couldn't see her face from a distance, he knew who it was. [1211] He would never see Lily again, he thought, but he wondered how long it would be before he met Mr. Quin again. He turned around, stepping on the lawn, and walked towards the tea table, towards the colorful tea set, towards his old friend Tom Addison. Beril would not return. He was certain of that. Mr. Duffton Kingsburn was unharmed.
[1212] A small black dog dashed across the lawn towards Mr. Satterthwaite. It stopped beside him, panting for a moment and wagging its tail. A note was tied around its neck with a ribbon. Mr. Satterthwaite bent down to retrieve it, unfolding it. The note read, "Congratulations! See you next time. H.Q."
[1213] "Thank you, Hermes," Mr. Satterthwaite said, watching the dog run back across the grass to the two figures. Only he knew they were there, but they were no longer visible.

[1215] * * *
[1216][1] The original text is "Harlequin", a character in Italian, British, and other comedy or pantomime performances who wears a mask, a colorful costume, and carries a wooden sword. The main character in the following story is named "Harley Quin", which is a translation of "Harlequin". Agatha borrowed this character to express her love for the clown. More stories about this character can be found in the book "The Mysterious Mr. Quinn" (New Star Press, 2017).
[1217][2] Refers to the last story in "The Mysterious Mr. Quinn" called "The Clown's Path".

[1460] Love Detective
[1461] Mr. Satterthwaite, a short and thoughtful man, looked at the gentleman of the house. The friendship between these two men was quite special. The Colonel was a simple country gentleman, whose greatest joy was in sports. He was quite reluctant to stay in London for a few weeks. Mr. Satterthwaite, on the other hand, was a city man. He was very familiar with French cuisine, women's fashion, and all the latest gossip. He was passionate about observing human nature and was an expert in his own field - an observer of life.
[1462] In stark contrast, the Colonel showed no interest in local gossip and avoided any kind of emotion. Thus, it seemed that there was little in common between Melrose Colonel and Mr. Satterthwaite. The two men could become friends mainly because their fathers were friends. Moreover, their social circles overlapped, and they both disliked nouveaux riche figures.
[1463] It was around 7:30 now. The two men were sitting in the Colonel's comfortable study, with the Colonel telling a thrilling and passionate story about a horse race last winter. Mr. Satterthwaite's knowledge of horse racing was limited to going to see the stables every Sunday morning in the old-fashioned country house, and his listening was merely out of politeness.
[1464] A piercing phone ring interrupted the Colonel's monologue. He walked to the desk, picked up the receiver.
[1465] "Hello? Yes, I am Colonel Melrose. Who is this?" His entire demeanor changed, becoming more composed and serious, as if an administrator rather than a sports enthusiast was speaking.
[1466] He listened for a moment and then replied briefly: "Okay, Curtis. I'll be right there." He put down the receiver and turned to his guest. "Someone has found James DeWitt, Sir, murdered in his study."
[1467] "What?" Mr. Satterthwaite was shocked and shaken.
[1468] "I must go to Alder Road immediately. Would you like to come with me?"
[1469] Mr. Satterthwaite remembered that the Colonel was the chief police officer of the county. "If I'm not in the way..." he hesitated.
[1470] "Don't worry. It was Constable Curtis who called just now. He's a good, honest lad, just not too bright. Mr. Satterthwaite, I would be delighted if you would come with me. I feel this case is quite tricky."
[1471] "Did they catch the killer?"
[1472] "No," Melrose replied briefly.
[1473] Mr. Satterthwaite's trained ears picked up a slight reservation in the brief denial. He began to search his mind for what he knew about the Dewitt family.
[1474] The late Sir James was a pompous old man, quick to anger and make enemies. He was in his sixties, with white hair and a ruddy complexion, and was notorious for his stinginess.
[1475] He continued to search his mind for an image of Lady Dewitt. She appeared to him, young, with reddish-brown hair and a slender figure. Various rumors and veiled hints also came to mind, which explained why Melrose looked so grim. He stood up, and his thoughts continued to flow.
[1476] Five minutes later, Mr. Satterthwaite climbed into the owner's two-seater car and sat beside him, driving off into the night.
[1477] The colonel was a taciturn man. They had driven about a mile and a half before he spoke. He said abruptly, "I suppose you know them?"
[1478] "The DeWitts, of course. I'm very familiar with them. I've only met Mr. DeWitt once, but I've seen Mrs. DeWitt quite often."

Her performance was very moving to me. She has no modern feel - she's a pure survivor of the old times. You can imagine her living in a governor's mansion or imagine her as Lucrezia Borgia."

[1566] Many of the desk's ornaments were in disarray and broken, with a particularly noticeable large and black enamel clock lying on its side in the center of the desk.
[1567] The inspector cleared his throat.
[1568] "That's what you might call luck, sir," he said. "You see, the clock stopped, at six thirty. It tells us when the crime was committed. It's so convenient."
[1569] The colonel stared at the clock.
[1570] "As you say," he said, "very convenient. " He paused for a moment before adding, "I don't like to think that way, Inspector."
[1571] He looked at the other two who had accompanied him, searching for Quin. His gaze pleaded.
[1572] "Damn it," he said, "it's too neat. You all know what I mean. It doesn't happen like this."
[1573] "You mean," Quin murmured, "the clock shouldn't have fallen like that?"
[1574] Melrose looked at him for a moment, then turned back to the clock. The clock looked pitifully innocent, as something that had been suddenly deprived of its dignity. Melrose Colonel carefully straightened it. He pounded the table, and the clock shook but did not tilt. Melrose struck again, and the clock tilted reluctantly, slowly toppling over.
[1575] "When was the murder discovered?" Melrose suddenly asked.
[1576] "Around seven o'clock, sir."
[1577] "Who discovered it?"
[1578] "The butler."
[1579] "Send him over," the police commissioner said. "I want to see him, and by the way, where is Mrs. DeWitt?"
[1580] "She's lying down, sir. Her maid said she was lying down and wouldn't see anyone."
[1581] Melrose nodded. Detective Inspector Curtis went to fetch the butler. Mr. Quin studied the fireplace thoughtfully. Mr. Satterthwaite also looked at the fireplace, observing the smoldering logs for a moment before something shining caught his attention. He bent down to pick up a small silver arc of glass.
[1582] "You wanted me, sir?"
[1583] The butler's voice was still trembling and indistinct. Mr. Satterthwaite let the glass fragment slide into his waistcoat pocket and turned around.
[1584] The old butler stood in the doorway.
[1585] "Sit down," the Chief Constable said kindly. "You're shaking all over. It seems to have quite frightened you."
[1586]“Yes, sir.”
[1587]“Well, I won't keep you long. Your master came back at a quarter to five, did he not?”
[1588]“Yes, sir. He asked me to bring him tea here. Later, when I came to take away the tea tray, he asked me to call for Jenkins – that's his personal servant, sir.”
[1589]“What time was that?”
[1590]“About six-fifteen, sir.”
[1591] "Ah – and then what?"
[1592] "I sent for Jenkins, sir. I didn't come back here until seven o'clock to prepare to close the windows and draw the curtains, and that's when I saw –"
[1593] Melrose interrupted him, saying, "Well, well, you don't have to tell me again. You didn't touch the body or move anything in the house, did you?"
[1594] "Oh! Certainly not, sir! I rushed off to call the police station as quickly as I could."
[1595] "And then what?"
[1596] "I told Miss—the lady's maid, sir—to inform the lady."
[1597] "Did you see your mistress at all during the evening?"
[1598] Colonel Melrose asked this question casually, but Mr. Satterthwaite's keen ears caught a hint of anxiety in his tone.
[1599] "No, sir. After the tragedy, the lady stayed in her own suite all evening."
[1600] "Had you seen her before that?"
[1601] The question was sudden, and everyone in the room noticed the butler's hesitation before he spoke.
[1602] "Sir, I—I only caught a glimpse of her, as she was going down the stairs."
[1603] "Did she come here?"
[1604] Mr. Satterthwaite held his breath.
[1605] "I—I think so, sir."
[1606] "What time was that?"
[1607] The room was so quiet that the sound of a pin dropping could be heard. Mr. Satterthwaite wondered if the old butler knew how to answer.
[1608] "Around six-thirty, sir."
[1609] Colonel Melrose took a deep breath. "That's all, thank you. Please tell Jenkins, the footman, to come see me."
[1610] Jenkins, a long-faced man with a sneaky air, appeared immediately at the summons.
[1611] Mr. Satterthwaite thought that if the man could get away with it, he would murder his master with ease.
[1612] He was eager to hear how the man would answer Colonel Melrose's question. However, his story was simple and straightforward. He had brought his master a pair of soft leather slippers and taken away the pair of brogues.
[1613] "What did you do after that, Jenkins?"
[1614] "I went back to the servants' quarters, sir."
[1615] "When did you leave your master?"
[1616] "Definitely past six fifteen, sir."
[1617] "Where were you at six thirty, Jenkins?"
[1618] "In the servant's room, sir."
[1619] Melrose Colonel nodded and dismissed the servant, then looked inquiringly at Curtis.
[1620] "Completely correct, sir. I investigated. He was in the servant's room from around six twenty to seven o'clock."
[1621] "So he's not a suspect then," the police commissioner said, somewhat disappointedly. "And there doesn't seem to be any other motive."
[1622] Everyone looked at each other.
[1623] There was a knock at the door.
[1624] "Come in," the colonel said.
[1625] A rather anxious-looking maid appeared at the door.
[1626] "The lady heard that Major Melrose is here, and she wanted to see him, may I ask him to come?"
[1627] "Of course, he can," Major Melrose said. "I'll be right there. Can you show me the way?"
[1628] Suddenly, a hand pushed the maid aside, and a completely different figure stood at the door. Laura DeWitt looked like a visitor from another world.
[1629] She wore a tight, old-fashioned navy blue brocade tea gown with no sleeves, her reddish-brown hair parted in the middle, with the sides covering her ears and a simple bun at the nape of her neck. Mrs. DeWitt was aware of her unique style, resting one arm on the door frame to steady herself and the other hanging by her side, holding a book. Mr. Satterthwaite thought she looked like the Virgin Mary in an early Italian painting.
[1630] She stood there, swaying slightly. Major Melrose rushed forward.
[1631] "I have come to tell you – to tell you –”
[1632] Her voice was low and round. Mr. Satterthwaite was so entranced by the artistry of the moment that he forgot he was in a crime scene.
[1633] "Wait, Mrs. DeWitt –” Colonel Melrose put an arm around her waist to steady her. He led her through the hall into a small sitting room, the walls of which were hung with faded silk tapestries. Quincannon and Mr. Satterthwaite followed. She sank wearily into a low couch, her head resting on a rust-colored cushion, her eyes closed. The three men looked at her. Suddenly she opened her eyes, sat up, and said in a very calm voice:
[1634] "I killed him. I have come to tell you – it was I who killed him!”
[1635] There was a moment of oppressive silence. Mr. Satterthwaite's heart missed a beat.
[1636] "Mrs. Dehewton," Melrose said, "you are greatly upset – your nerves are on edge. I believe you do not know what you are saying."
[1637] Will she retract her statement – since there is still time?
[1638] "I know exactly what I am saying. I shot him."
[1639] Two men in the room gasped, the third remained silent.
[1640] Laura Dehewton leaned forward, motionless.
[1641] "You still don't understand? I shot him downstairs. I've admitted it."
[1642] The book she had been holding fell to the floor. Inside it was a paper knife, shaped like a dagger with a gem-encrusted hilt. Superintendent Sattthwaite picked up the paper knife and placed it on the table. As he did so, he thought, What a dangerous tool—it could be used to kill someone.
[1643] "Well—" Laura Dodd's voice sounded impatient. "What are you going to do with me? Arrest me? Take me away?"
[1644] Colonel Melrose spoke haltingly.
[1645] "This is very serious, Mrs. Dodd. I must ask you to return to your room until I, er, make some arrangements."
[1646] She nodded and stood up, looking very calm and composed, grave and stern.
[1647] She was turning towards the door when Mr. Quincannon spoke, "What did you do with the pistol, Mrs. DeWitt?"
[1648] A tremor flitted across her face. "I, I dropped it on the floor of the room. No, I think I threw it out the window — oh! I can't remember. What difference does it make? I can't even remember what I've done. It's not important, is it?"
[1649] "No," Mr. Quincannon said, "I don't think it's very important."
[1650] She looked at him curiously, her expression showing some alarm. Then she suddenly turned and hurried out of the room. Mr. Satterthwaite hurried after her. He had a feeling she might faint, but she had reached the middle of the stairs and did not show any sign of weakness. The anxious-looking maid stood at the foot of the stairs, and Mr. Satterthwaite spoke to her in a commanding tone, "Take care of the lady."
[1651] "Yes, sir," the maid prepared to climb the stairs to catch up with the figure in the blue gown, "Oh, please tell me, sir, they don't suspect him, do they?"
[1652] "Suspect who?"
[1653] "Jennings, sir. Oh! To be honest, sir, he wouldn't hurt a fly."
[1654] "Jennings? No, of course not. Take care of your mistress!"
[1655] "Yes, sir."
[1656] The maid quickly ascended the stairs. Mr. Satterthwaite returned to the previous parlor.
[1657] Colonel Melrose sighed heavily. "Blast, things are more complicated than they seem. That... that was like something a woman would do in a novel."
[1658] "Not quite real," Mr. Satterthwaite agreed with his assessment, "Like a play on stage."
[1659] Mr. Queene nodded. "Indeed, you enjoyed the show, didn't you? You're the type to appreciate a good performance."
[1660] Mr. Satterthwaite glared at him sharply.
[1661] Suddenly, there was a sound in the distance.
[1662] "Sounds like a gunshot," Colonel Melrose said. "I think it was the gamekeeper who fired it. Perhaps, she heard that sound. Perhaps she came down to see what was happening. And she wouldn't go near to examine the body, she would make a quick conclusion –"
[1663] "Mr. Druva has arrived, sir," the butler said, standing apologetically at the door.
[1664] "Oh?" Colonel Melrose asked. "What is it?"
[1665] "Mr. Druva has arrived, sir, and he wants to speak with you, is that alright?"
[1666] Colonel Melrose leaned back in his chair. "Let him in," he said sternly.
[1667] Soon, Paul Danglars stood at the door. As Colonel Melrose had hinted, there was something very un-English about him - his elegant bearing, his handsome, dark complexion, and his eyes that were a bit too close together. He had an air of the Renaissance about him. He gave the same impression as Laura de Wint.
[1668] "Good evening, gentlemen," said Danglars. His slightly theatrical bow was somewhat exaggerated.
[1669] "I don't know what you're here for, Mr. Danglars," Colonel Melrose said sharply. "If it's not about this case -"
[1670] Danglars smiled and interrupted him. "On the contrary," he said. "It's very much about this case."
[1671] "What do you mean?" Colonel Melrose asked.
[1672] "I mean," de Rance answered calmly, "that I'm here to confess. I killed Sir James de Witt."
[1673] "Do you know what you're saying?" Colonel Melrose asked gravely.
[1674] "Absolutely," de Rance replied.
[1675] The young man stared intently at the table.
[1676] "I don't understand..."
[1677] "Don't understand why I turned myself in? Call it regret—say whatever you like. I stabbed him, in the heart—you know that as well as I do. He pointed to the table, "I saw you found the weapon. A convenient little tool. Lady Dedwinton accidentally put it between the pages of a book, and I just grabbed it—"
[1678] "Wait a moment," Colonel Melrose said, "Are you admitting that you killed Sir James with this knife?"
[1679] "Exactly. I snuck in through the window. He had his back to me, and it was easy. I left the room the same way I came in."
[1680] "Through the window?"
[1681] "Yes, through the window, of course."
[1682] "At what time?"
[1683] Danglars hesitated. "Let me think—I was chatting with the gamekeeper—that was at 6:15. I heard the church bell. It must have been, uh, about 6:30."
[1684] A cold smile played on the colonel's lips.
[1685] "Indeed, young man," he said, "it was 6:30. Perhaps you've heard about this time? This, is a very peculiar case of murder!"
[1686] "Why?"
[1687] "So many people have come forward," Colonel Melrose said.
[1688] They heard the young man's sharp intake of breath.
[1689] "Who else has come forward?" He tried to keep his voice steady but failed.
[1690] "Mrs. DeWitt."
[1691] DeLange turned his head away and forced a laugh. "Mrs. DeWitt is easily hysterical," he said lightly. "I wouldn't take her word for it."
[1692] "I don't think I would either," said Melrose. "But there's something strange about this murder case."
[1693] "What's strange?"
[1694] "It's like this," said Melrose. "Mrs. DeWitt admits to shooting James sir, but you admit to stabbing him. However, both of you are lucky. He wasn't shot or stabbed to death. His head was crushed."
[1695] "Good heavens!" DeLange exclaimed. "But a woman couldn't have done that—"
[1696] He stopped, biting his lip. Melrose nodded, with a smile.
[1697] "It's often read about in books," he murmured to himself, "but I've never seen it before."
[1698] "What?"
[1699] "A pair of young fools both accuse themselves of being the murderer because they both think the other did something stupid. Now we have to start all over again."
[1700] "The valet," said Mr. Satterthwaite loudly, "the maid just now—I didn't pay attention. She was afraid we suspected the valet. So he must have had some motive, which we don't know, but she must have known."
[1701] Colonel Melrose frowned and pressed the bell. When someone answered, he instructed, "Ask Lady DeWitt if she could come over again."
[1702] They waited quietly, and she finally arrived. Seeing DeLong, she shuddered and extended a hand to steady herself. Colonel Melrose rushed over to help her.
[1703] "Everything is fine, Lady DeWitt. Please don't worry."
[1704] "I don't understand. What is Mr. DeLong doing here?"
[1705] DeLong approached her. "Laura, Laura, why did you do that?"
[1706] "Do what?"
[1707] "I know. It was for me. You thought... well, it was all so natural, I suppose. But oh! You angel!"
[1708] Colonel Melrose coughed. He was not a man who liked to show emotions and feared dramatic scenes.
[1709] "If I may say so, Mrs. Dwyte, you and Mr. Dreville are both fortunate not to be suspects. He just admitted to being the killer—oh, nothing serious, he didn't kill anyone! However, we would like to know the truth of the matter, so let's not beat around the bush. The butler said you went to the study at 6:30—is that correct?"
[1710] Laura glanced at Dreville, who nodded in confirmation.
[1711] "The truth, Laura," he said. "We need to know the truth now."
[1712] She took a deep breath. "I will tell you."
[1713] Mr. Satterthwaite quickly pushed a chair over for her, and she sat down.
[1714] "I did go downstairs. I opened the study door and saw—"
[1715] She paused, composing herself. Mr. Satterthwaite leaned over and patted her hand encouragingly.
[1716] "Yes," he said, "yes. You saw —"
[1717] "My husband was lying on the desk. I saw his head... blood... ah!"
[1718] She buried her face in her hands. The police commissioner moved closer.
[1719] "I'm sorry, Mrs. Dethridge. You thought Mr. Danglars shot him?"
[1720] She nodded. "Forgive me, Paul," she pleaded. "But you said — you said —"
[1721] "I would have shot him like a dog," DeLancey said gloomily, "I remember. That day I found him beating you."
[1722] The chief of police controlled the conversation.
[1723] "So, I see, Mrs. Dudley, please go up to your room again, uh, don't say anything. We won't talk about why you did it. Did you touch the body or come near the desk?"
[1724] She shuddered. "No, no. I ran out of the room right away."
[1725] "I understand, I understand. What was the exact time then? Do you know?"
[1726] "I got back to the bedroom at six thirty."
[1727] "So, James was dead by six twenty-five. The clock - it's a fake, huh? We've always suspected this. There's nothing easier than stopping the clock at the time you want by moving the hands. But they made a mistake by letting the clock fall down like that. Well, it looks like the suspects are narrowed down to the butler or the valet. I don't believe it's the butler. Tell me, Mrs. DeWinton, what could have made Jennings hate your husband?"
[1728] Laura lifted her face from her hands. "Well, it's not really hatred, but - oh, James told me this morning that he was being fired. My husband found out he was stealing."
[1729] "Hmm! We're getting a clearer picture now. Jennings would have been fired for his bad behavior, which was serious for him."
[1730] "You mentioned the clock," said Laura DeWinton. "It was just a coincidence - if you wanted to know the time - James would have his little golf watch with him. It didn't break when he fell forward, did it?"
[1731] "Good point," said the Colonel slowly. "But I'm afraid – Curtis!"
[1732] The Inspector nodded in understanding and left the room. Soon, he returned. In his outstretched palm was a silver watch, about the size of a golf ball. These watches are sold exclusively to golfers, who usually carry them and the ball together in their pockets.
[1733] "Here you go, sir," he said. "But I'm afraid it's probably no use. These watches are too hard."
[1734] The Colonel took the watch from his hand and held it to his ear.
[1735] "Well, it seems to have stopped," he said.
[1736] He pressed his thumb, and the cover opened, shattering the glass face.
[1737] "Ah!" he exclaimed, delighted.
[1738] The hands were pointing exactly at six fifteen.
[1739] "A fine bottle of Bordeaux, Colonel Melrose," said Mr. Queene.
[1740] At nine thirty, the three men had just finished a late dinner at Colonel Melrose's house. Mr. Satterthwaite was particularly animated.
[1741] "I was right, wasn't I?" he chuckled. "You can't deny, Mr. Quin, that your appearance tonight saved two foolish young people from hanging themselves."
[1742] "Me?" said Mr. Quin. "Certainly not. I did nothing."
[1743] "Well, since the result is already settled, it wouldn't have mattered," said Mr. Satterthwaite in agreement. "But it very nearly happened. I'll never forget when Mrs. Duddleswell said, 'I killed him.' I've seen less on the stage than half of what I saw today."
[1744] "I tend to agree with you," said Mr. Quin.
[1745] "It's almost unbelievable that such a thing could happen in real life outside of a novel," Colonel Melrose remarked for what seemed like the twentieth time that evening.
[1746] "Is that so?" said Mr. Quin.
[1747] The colonel looked at him and said, "Damn it, it happened tonight."
[1748] "Just to remind you," Mr. Satterthwaite leaned back and sipped his Bordeaux, "Mrs. Dwyatt was very high-minded, very high-minded, but she made a mistake. She should not have hastily concluded that her husband was shot. Similarly, Mr. Dulong merely because he saw the knife on the table in front of us, foolishly assumed that he was stabbed to death. Mrs. Dwyatt brought the knife down with her by coincidence."
[1749] "Is that so?" asked Mr. Quin.
[1750] "What if they had simply admitted to killing James Sir, without specifying how?" Mr. Satterthwaite continued. "What would have happened then?"
[1751] "Their confession might have been accepted," said Mr. Quin with a strange smile.
[1752] "The whole thing was like a novel," said the colonel.
[1753] "I dare say they got their inspiration from a novel," said Mr. Quin.
[1754] "Perhaps," agreed Mr. Satterthwaite, "what one reads has a strange way of showing up in one's behavior." He looked at Mr. Quin. "Of course," he said, "the clock from the start aroused suspicion. You know, it's so easy to move the hands of a clock or a watch forward or backward!"
[1755] Mr. Quin nodded, repeating the last few words. "Forward," he paused, "backward."
[1756] There was something inspiring in his voice. His dark, bright eyes fixed on Mr. Satterthwaite.
[1757] "The clock's hands were moved forward," Mr. Satterthwaite said. "We know that."
[1758] "Is that so?" Mr. Queene asked.
[1759] Mr. Satterthwaite looked at him. "Do you mean," he said slowly, "that someone moved the hands backward? But that doesn't make sense. It's impossible."
[1760] "Not impossible," Mr. Queene mumbled.
[1761] "This - this is absurd. Who would benefit from this?"
[1762] "I think it would only benefit someone who had an alibi for that time period."
[1763] "Good heavens!" exclaimed the colonel. "At that time, young Danglars said he was talking to the gamekeeper."
[1764] "He told us that quite clearly," said Mr. Satterthwaite.
[1765] They looked at each other. They felt uneasy, as if the solid ground beneath their feet was giving way. Facts were shifting and changing, taking on new angles and unfamiliar faces. At the center of this kaleidoscope was Mr. Quin's dark, smiling face.
[1766] "But if that's the case -" Melrose began to say.
[1767] Mr. Satterthwaite was quick to finish his sentence. "The whole thing would be turned on its head. The setup is the same, but it points to the valet. Oh, it's impossible! Impossible. So why did they all admit to committing the murder?"
[1768] "Yes," said Mr. Queene. "Until then, you were still suspecting them, weren't you?" He continued, his voice calm and gentle. "Colonel, you said it was like a plot from a book. They did indeed draw inspiration from a book. They acted out the actions of the innocent male and female protagonists. Of course, such actions would make you think they were innocent - because they had the power of tradition behind them. Mr. Satterthwaite kept saying it was like a stage play. You two were both right. What we are facing is not the truth. Unknowingly, but you both kept saying it. If they wanted us to believe it, they should have told a more perfect story."
[1769] The two men looked at him, bewildered.
[1770] "That would have been a smarter move," said Mr. Satterthwaite slowly. "That would have been quite a smart move. Also, I've been thinking about something else. The butler said he came in at seven to close the windows, so he must have thought the windows were open."
[1771] "Dulwich was the one who climbed in through the window," said Mr. Queer. "He knocked out James, then helped the lady doctor stage the scene -"
[1772] He looked at Mr. Satterthwaite encouragingly, and the latter began to piece together the puzzle:
[1773] "They broke the clock, put it sideways on the table. Yes, they wound the clock and broke the watch. Then, he jumped out the window and escaped, and she locked the window. But there's one thing I don't understand. Why didn't they just wind the clock and break the watch instead of bothering to break the clock and the watch?"
[1774] "The clock was too obvious," said Mr. Queer. "Anyone would have seen through such an obvious disguise."
[1775] "But the watch thing is too far-fetched. Hey, we only thought of that watch by chance."
[1776] "Oh, no," said Mr. Quincannon. "That was Mrs. DeWitt's idea. Remember."
[1777] Satterthwaite stared at him.
[1778] "And, you know," Mr. Quincannon said softly, "you can't ignore the fact that the valet is the one who would know what's in his master's pockets. If D'Anglars moved the clock's hands, the valet would have moved the watch's hands. They just didn't understand the secrets of human nature. They're not like you, Satterthwaite."
[1779] Satterthwaite shook his head.
[1780] "I was completely wrong," he muttered humbly. "I thought you were here to save them."
[1781] "I have," said Mr. Queene, "not them two, but others. Perhaps you didn't notice Mrs. Duddleswell's maid? She didn't wear blue satin, and she didn't play a part in the play. But she's a very nice girl, and I think she's very fond of Jennings. I think one of you two can save her sweetheart from the gallows."
[1782] "We have no evidence," said Colonel Melrose heavily.
[1783] Mr. Queene smiled. "Mr. Satterthwaite has."
[1784] "Me?" Mr. Satterthwaite was surprised.
[1785] Mr. Queene continued, "You have one piece of evidence that proves the watch wasn't broken in James' pocket. You can't break a watch like that without opening the case. Try it and see. Someone took the watch out, opened the case, moved the hands back, broke the glass face, closed the case, and put it back in the dead man's pocket. No one noticed the missing piece of glass."
[1786] "Oh!" Mr. Satterthwaite exclaimed. He quickly reached into his waistcoat pocket and pulled out a piece of curved glass.
[1787] At this moment, he felt very pleased with himself.
[1788] "With this," said Mr. Satterthwaite in a self-satisfied tone, "I will save a man from the brink of death."

[1791] [1] Lucrezia Borgia (1480-1519), the illegitimate daughter of Pope Alexander VI. She came from a noble family and long sponsored artists for their artistic endeavors, making her one of the behind-the-scenes supporters of the European Renaissance.
[1792] [2] Refers to the first story in "The Mysterious Mr. Quin," titled "Mr. Quin's Arrival."
[1793] Accompanied by a dog
[1794] The woman sitting behind the desk at the employment agency cleared her throat and looked at the girl across from her.
[1795] "So you're not considering this job?" she said. "This is a job that just came in this morning. I think it's a very good Italian family, a widow with a three-year-old boy, and an elderly woman, probably her mother or aunt."
[1796] Joyce Lambert shook her head.
[1797] "I can't leave England," her voice was weary, "I have my reasons. Can you help me find a day job?"
[1798] Her voice trembled slightly - she had to exert herself to keep it so slight. Her sapphire eyes pleaded with the woman across from her.
[1799] "That's not easy, Mrs. Lambert. Day jobs are mostly for nannies, but they require a complete set of credentials. And you don't have any. My register has over a hundred sets of credentials, not exaggerating, over a hundred.” She paused, "Is it because you have someone at home that you can't leave?"
[1800] Joyce nodded.
[1801] "Do you have a child?"
[1802] "No, I don't," a weak smile played on her face.
[1803] "Well, that's a shame. I'll do my best, but -"
[1804] The interview ended. Joyce stood up. As she walked from the dirty office to the street, she bit her lip, trying to hold back the tears that threatened to well up.
[1805] "You can't cry," she sternly admonished herself, "Don't be a silly little girl. You're in a state of panic - that's your state - panic. Panic doesn't help. It's still early morning, and many good things may happen. Aunt Mary should kindly take you in for two weeks. Come on, girl, take a step, don't keep your kind relatives waiting."
[1806] She walked down Euston Road, through the park, to Victoria Street. There she turned into a "Navy and Army Store." She walked into the bar, sat down, and looked at her watch. It was exactly 1:30 PM. Five minutes later, an old lady with a bundle of packages sat down beside her.
[1807] "Ah! You're here, Joyce. I'm afraid I'm a little late. The service in the dining room isn't as good as it used to be. Have you had lunch already?"
[1808] Joyce hesitated for a minute or two before quietly saying, "Yes, I have. Thank you."
[1809] "I always have lunch at 12:30," Aunt Mary said, arranging the packages and settling comfortably. "Not in a hurry, and the air is fresh. The curry eggs here are very good."
[1810] "Really?" Joyce said weakly. She couldn't think about the curry eggs—steaming hot and smelling delicious! She refused to let herself think about it.
[1811] "Child, you look pale," Aunt Mary said, looking very plump herself. "Don't be trendy and go without meat; it's all nonsense. Eating a bit of meat won't do you any harm."
[1812] Joyce interrupted her rambling, "Yes, it won't do any harm." She hoped Aunt Mary wouldn't talk about food anymore. Meeting her at 1:30, filling her with hope for lunch, and then going on about curried eggs and sliced roast beef—it was too cruel—too cruel.
[1813] "Well, dear," Aunt Mary said, "I received your letter—it's so nice to be able to trust you. I said that I would always be happy to see you, and I should have—but unfortunately, I just rented the house out at a very good price. It was too good to pass up. [1817] "You look quite weak, child," said Aunt Mary, looking at her closely. "You're too thin, all skin and bone. You used to look so healthy, what's happened to you? Your complexion was always rosy and healthy. You should exercise more."

[2052] * *
[2053] [1] Albert Jacobsz Cuyp (1620-1691), a Dutch landscape painter, is famous for his depictions of rural tranquil life.
[2054] Plum Blossom
[2055] 1
[2056] Vincent Easton is waiting under the big clock at Victoria Station. He is restless and occasionally glances up at the clock, thinking, "How many men have waited here for a woman who stood them up?" He feels a pang in his heart. What if she changes her mind and doesn't come? Women do that, he thinks. Does he have faith in her? Has he ever had faith in her? Does he know everything about her? Hasn't she been causing him doubts from the very beginning? He seems to know two women – one is the young and smiling wife of Richard Darrel, and the other is always silent and mysterious, who used to walk with him in the garden of Haidme Manor. She is like a plum blossom – that's how he has always imagined her – perhaps because they had that intoxicating and unbelievable first kiss under a plum tree. The air is fresh and filled with the fragrance of plum blossoms. She lies on her back, her face as smooth, soft, and silent as a plum blossom, with a few soft and fragrant petals falling on it. Plum blossom – strange, fragrant, mysterious.
[2057] That was two weeks ago – the day after he met her. And now, he is waiting for her to arrive and be with her forever. He feels another pang in his heart. She won't come. How could he believe she would come? It would require giving up too much. The beautiful Mrs. Darrel would never do such a thing. It would become a sensation, a scandal that would be talked about and never forgotten. There are better and more appropriate ways to do it – like getting a careful divorce.

I believe they'll think he shot you. Anyway, I don't care. It's all so much fun. All just a game! That woman in London, see how she looked when she recognized me. And that silly woman this morning!”
[3284] He nodded.
[3285] At that moment, a clear whistle sounded, sending shivers down the spine. Someone was whistling the tune of "Three Blind Mice."
[3286] Trotter jumped, his gun wavering. A voice called out, "Down, Mrs. Davis. Stay low."
[3287] Molly dropped to the floor, hiding behind the sofa on the side of the room where Metcalf Major leapt out and tackled Trotter. The gun went off, the bullet embedding itself in an ordinary oil painting, a beloved portrait of the late Miss Emery.
[3288] Chaos ensued as Jules rushed in, followed closely by Christopher and Signor Paravicini.
[3289] Metcalf Major held Trotter firmly, speaking succinctly:
[3290] "I slipped in while you were playing the piano, hiding behind the sofa. I've been watching him all along—that is, I knew he wasn't a policeman. I am the policeman—Detective Inspector Taner. We agreed with Metcalf that I would take his place. Scotland Yard thought it would be better to have someone else. Well, old chap—" he spoke gently to the now subdued Trotter, "you come with me. No one will harm you. We'll take care of you."
[3291] The copper-skinned young man asked in a pitiful childish voice, "Won't George be angry with me?"
[3292] Metcalf said, "No, George won't be angry."
[3293] As he passed Giles, he whispered, "He's gone mad, poor fellow."
[3294] They all went out together. Pallavicini touched Christopher Raine's arm.
[3295] "You, my friend," he said, "come with me."
[3296] Only Jules and Molly were left, gazing into each other's eyes. For a moment, the couple embraced.
[3297] "Dear," Jules said, "are you sure he didn't hurt you?"
[3298] "No, no, I'm fine. Jules, I was so stupid. I thought you – why did you go to London?"
[3299] "Dear, tomorrow is our wedding anniversary, and I wanted to get you a gift without you knowing."
[3300] "How coincidental! I went to London to get you a gift, and I didn't want you to know either."
[3301] "I was so jealous of that nutcase. I was so angry. Forgive me, darling."
[3302] The door opened, and Mr. Paravicini bounded in, grinning from ear to ear. "I'm interrupting your reconciliation... what a lovely sight. But, oh, I must be going. A police jeep has managed to make its way here, and I need to convince them to take me with them."
[3303] He bent down and whispered conspiratorially into Molly's ear, "I might be a little embarrassed in a few days – but I'm sure I can handle it. If you receive a box – with a goose, a turkey, some foie gras, a ham, and a few pairs of nylon stockings – you'll know it's my little tribute to such a charming lady. Mr. Davis, my bill is on the table in the lobby."
[3304] He kissed Molly's hand and bounced out the door.
[3305] "Nylon? Foie gras? Who is Mr. Paravicini? Is he Santa Claus?"
[3306] "Probably some black market type," Jules said.
[3307] Christopher Lane timidly poked his head in. "Dear ones," he said, "I hope I'm not interrupting, but there's a rather unpleasant smell coming from the kitchen. Should I do something about it?"
[3308] Molly groaned. "My pie!" She hurried out of the room.
[3309] (Wang Zhanyi translation)
